        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29125723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8840156093804050876">Totally <b>new review</b> (replacing ancient, short, less favourable one).<br/>Orlando. or-LAN-do. Wrap your tongue around it, and whisper it. There’s a luscious, syrupy, sensual, mysterious feel. Much like the eponymous hero(ine), and the sumptuously described natural and man-made world Orlando inhabits.<br/><br/>The name conjures cross-dressing disguises in Shakespeare’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42607.As_You_Like_It' title='As You Like It by William Shakespeare' rel='nofollow noopener'>As You Like It</a>, a <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/862612.Orlando_the_Marmalade_Cat' rel='nofollow noopener'>Marmalade Cat</a>, maybe <a href='https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0107756/?ref_=fn_al_tt_1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tilda Swinton</a> or <a href='https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0167260/characters/nm0089217?ref_=tt_cl_t8' rel='nofollow noopener'>Legolas</a>, and, for Google, theme parks in Florida. If you know the novel’s USP and Greek my</span>
  <span id="freeText8840156093804050876" style="display:none">Totally <b>new review</b> (replacing ancient, short, less favourable one).<br>Orlando. or-LAN-do. Wrap your tongue around it, and whisper it. There’s a luscious, syrupy, sensual, mysterious feel. Much like the eponymous hero(ine), and the sumptuously described natural and man-made world Orlando inhabits.<br><br>The name conjures cross-dressing disguises in Shakespeare’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42607.As_You_Like_It" title="As You Like It by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">As You Like It</a>, a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/862612.Orlando_the_Marmalade_Cat" rel="nofollow noopener">Marmalade Cat</a>, maybe <a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0107756/?ref_=fn_al_tt_1" rel="nofollow noopener">Tilda Swinton</a> or <a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0167260/characters/nm0089217?ref_=tt_cl_t8" rel="nofollow noopener">Legolas</a>, and, for Google, theme parks in Florida. If you know the novel’s USP and Greek mythology, you may also think of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tiresias" rel="nofollow noopener">Tiresias</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metamorphoses" rel="nofollow noopener">Ovid’s Metamorphoses</a>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1541876409i/26575138._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>Tilda Swinton as Orlando, leaning against an oak tree, the title of Orlando’s lifelong poem</i> (<a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0107756/mediaviewer/rm2855666176" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br>My first encounters with Woolf were not positive. I didn’t “get” <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59716.To_the_Lighthouse" title="To the Lighthouse by Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">To the Lighthouse</a> in 2008. Orlando fared a little better shortly after. Last year, I read <b>Night and Day</b> (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1749373515" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>) and gained confidence to read more Woolf.<br><br><b>The Sex Thing - is not the only thing</b><br><br>I reread Orlando because in recent years, I’ve been dabbling in books that explore gender (see my shelf <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=sexuality-gender" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>). That reflects shifts in society as well as my own family.<br><br>But despite the famous and definite opening line, <i>“He - for there could be no doubt about his sex</i>”, readers shouldn’t obsess about it. One aspect I love about Orlando, and also Jeffrey Eugenides’ <b>Middlesex</b>, is that the switch of sex, though vital, is just one of many facets. In Middlesex, there are a dozen other types of transition (listed in my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24732884" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>).<br><br>Orlando’s diversity comes from genre and content. It is a pre-postmodern magical-realist mashup that slips effortlessly between fictionalised biography (with the biographer reporting their frustrations to the reader); pontifciations on high society; homage to, quotes from, and satire of famous writers and critics; queer feminist tract; numerous parallels with the detailed history of Vita Sackville-West's family and home (Knole); the pains of love sought and lost; streams of consciousness; comical wooing; the inspiration, methods, and frustrations of writing, especially for women; the emptiness of wealth (shades of <b>Gatsby</b>, which I reviewed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/389199058" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>); sensuous descriptions of nature, clothes, and furnishings; and it’s all wrapped up with an almost trippy ending.<br><br><b>The Plot</b><br><br>“<i>Time passed… and nothing whatever happened.</i>”<br><br>This is not primarily a plot-driven novel, and for Orlando, time is as flexible as sex/gender. <br><br>"<i>He would go out after breakfast a man of thirty and come home to dinner a man of fifty five at least. Some weeks added a century to his age, others no more than three seconds.</i>" <br><br>Orlando is a teenage nobleman in the court of Elizabeth I. He communes with nature and writes prolifically. He has lovers of both/ambiguous sexes, and adventures with Russians, Turks, and gypsies. Decades later, but only around 30 years old, Orlando awakes as a woman. This is barely mentioend by her or others, nor the fact she lives for another 300 years without aging noticeably. No explanation is sought or suggested. Its relevance is limited to observing the differing constraints on women through the ages, and offstage legal battles to inherit what only a man can inherit. <br><br>“<i>She need neither fight her age, nor submit to it. She was of it, yet remained herself.</i>”<br>(Here, “age” refers to historical period, rather than number of birthdays.)<br><br><b>If it’s not about Orlando’s sex or the plot, what IS it about?</b><br><br>Orlando’s core character and interests are consistent: nature, literature, and later, a quest for “life and a lover”. <br><br>In terms of sex, Orlando is the essence of fluidity, embracing all aspects of both (Woolf takes a binary view) in herself and her lovers: the differences are simultaneously profound and unremarked. When Orlando first realises he is now she, “she showed no surprise”.<br><br>“<i>His form combined in one the strength of a man and a woman’s grace… Orlando had become a woman… But in every other respect, Orlando remained precisely as he had been. The change of sex, though it altered their future, did nothing whatever to alter their identity.</i>”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1542006149i/26581906._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>One from the series "Human Metamorphosis" by Taylor James</i> (<a href="https://mymodernmet.com/human-metamorphosis-15-pics/" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br>For me, that’s the essential message of Orlando: <b>be true to yourself, regardless of externally defined labels</b>. That applies as much to the genre-defying book, as to Orlando the person. Labels can be useful, but they should only ever be <b>de</b>scriptive, not <b>pre</b>scriptive. <br><br>“<i>Different though the sexes are, they intermix. In every human being a vacillation from one sex to the other takes place, and often it is only the clothes that keep the male or female likeness.</i>”<br><br>Orlando changes outwardly, but before and after, over the centuries, Orlando is always a colourful, fluid mix. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1542012337i/26582091._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>A violet marbled end paper from a Folio Society book.</i> (Short video of it being made <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=qwkkIfldRjg/" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.)<br><br>“<i>He had eyes like drenched violets.</i>”<br><br>This book is famously a love-letter to Vita, but it’s suffused with violets: Orlando’s eyes; flowers, obviously; but also clouds of autumn; shades; shadows, and (unstated) Trefusis - Vita’s previous lover.<br><br><b>Quotes</b><br><br>“Illusions are to the soul what atmosphere is to the earth.” <br><br>“Russia where the sunsets are longer, the dawns less sudden, and sentences often left unfinished.”<br><br>“Memory is a seamstress, and a capricious one at that.”<br><br>“Society is everything and society is nothing.” (Sounds like Wilde.)<br><br><b>Quotes about Literature</b><br>•	“Now all young writers were in the pay of the booksellers and poured out any trash that would sell.” In the 16th/17th century.<br><br>•	“While fame impedes and constricts, obscurity wraps about a man like a mist.”<br><br>•	"Material luxury evaporated like so much sea mist under the miasma. So it was, and Orlando would sit by himself, reading, a naked man."<br><br>•	“Surely, since she is a woman, and a beautiful woman, and a woman in the prime of life, she will soon give over this pretence of writing and thinking and begin at least to think of a gamekeeper.”<br><br><b>Quotes about Love</b><br>•	“As he looked the thickness in his blood melted; the ice turned to wine in his veins; he heard the waters flowing and birds singing.” Suddenly lovestruck. Then bathos: he merely asks her to pass the salt.<br><br>•	“Orlando heard… far off the beating of Love’s wings. The distant stir of that soft plumage roused in him a thousand memories of rushing waters, of loveliness in the snow and faithlessness in the flood.”<br><br>•	“Love… has two faces; one white, the other black; two bodies; one smooth, the other hairy… each one is the exact opposite of the other. Yet, so strictly are they joined together that you cannot separate them.”<br><br>•	“Nothing.. is more heavenly than to resist and to yield; to yield and to resist.”<br><br>•	“She was married, true; but if one's husband was always sailing round Cape Horn, was it marriage? If one liked him, was it marriage? If one liked other people, was it marriage? And finally, <b>if one still wished, more than anything in the whole world, to write poetry, was it marriage?</b>”<br><br>•	“But love - as the male novelists define it - and who, after all, speak with greater authority? - has nothing whatever to do with kindness, fidelity, generosity, or poetry. Love is slipping off one's petticoat and - But we all know what love is.”<br><br><b>Quotes about Weather/Seasons</b><br>Significant changes are marked by dramatic weather:<br><br>•	“Everything was different. The weather itself... of another temper altogether. The brilliant amorous day was divided as sheerly from the night as land from water. Sunsets were redder and more intense; dawns were whiter and more auroral. Of our crepuscular half-lights lingering twilights they knew nothing. The rain fell vehemently, or not at all.”<br><br>•	“The equable but confused light of a summer’s morning in which everything is seen but nothing is seen distinctly.”<br><br>•	“The sun… was so girt about with clouds and the air was so saturated with water, that its beams were discolored and purples, oranges, and reds of a dull sort took the place of the more positive landscapes… Under this bruised and sullen canopy the green of the cabbages was less intense, and the white of the snow was muddied.”<br><br><b>Quotes about Clothes</b><br>•	“She fell to thinking what an odd pass we have come to when all a woman’s beauty has to be kept covered lest a sailor may fall from a mast-head.” Relevant today for debates about burqas, victim-blaming, and rape culture.<br><br>•	“Clothes… change our view of the world and the world’s view of us.” <br><br>•	“It is clothes that wear us and not we them.”<br><br>•	“Clothes are but a symbol of something hid deep beneath.” The very definition of a <b>sacrament</b>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8840156093804050876" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_661856300" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/661856300" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/661856300?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 1853262390" href="/book/show/824212.Orlando">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dolors by: </span>
      My <i>Woolfish</i> hunger
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer661856300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10217753817572630940"><i>Orlando</i> might have been devised as a mere divertimento, as a playful attempt to challenge the established views on sexuality or as a fantastical tale to confront the history of East and West by questioning the boundaries of space and time, but to this reader this novella meant much more. It meant a universe of fluctuating moods, characters and sweeping poetry that gives reason to be through the act of reading.<br/><br/>How to describe the nuanced melody of finely threaded irony prodigiously in tune with t</span>
  <span id="freeText10217753817572630940" style="display:none"><i>Orlando</i> might have been devised as a mere divertimento, as a playful attempt to challenge the established views on sexuality or as a fantastical tale to confront the history of East and West by questioning the boundaries of space and time, but to this reader this novella meant much more. It meant a universe of fluctuating moods, characters and sweeping poetry that gives reason to be through the act of reading.<br /><br />How to describe the nuanced melody of finely threaded irony prodigiously in tune with the most sophisticated sense of humor that entertains and prickles and urges to see the world without the limiting lenses of gender, class or social convention?<br />One can evolve unhindered when he suspends judgement and allows the flow of writing to give way to a solid account that sparkles because undeniable reality is better understood through the theatrical fiction of its form.<br /><br />How to account for centuries expanding and contracting beyond human comprehension, decades that amount to <i>the fall of a rose leaf on the ground</i>, years that disappear in a flash? <br />The passage of time is of no consequence when love for the written word equals the all-consuming passion for the person who knows us best regardless of clothing or hair style, manners or social rituals that distract us from the true essence of our beings. <br /><br />How to explain the ache spanning countless generations, eras and customs that is nestled in the heart of the artist who relishes the young, supple body, pure as driven snow; the fleeting grass under a blanket of blue or the stars reflected in pools of stagnant water both in London and Turkey?<br />Emily Dickinson says in her poem #466:<br /><i>For Occupation – This –<br />The spreading wide my narrow Hands<br />To gather Paradise - </i><br /><br />For Occupation – Writing – <br />Orlando claims a “room of her own” to write her life, a task that will also define her love, and infuse wholeness into the swelling tides that toss her multiple beings, her male and female groundings.<br />The result, be it an experimental biography, an unorthodox love declaration or a thought-provoking <i>roman à clef</i> to defy categorization at all levels, goes beyond its original purpose and becomes a fluid, ever-changing tapestry of voices answering other voices, speaking the universal language of poetry.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10217753817572630940" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_137132662" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137132662" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/137132662?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=bloomsbury">bloomsbury</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=english-novels">english-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137132662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13109657600348353106">I first read this many years ago; before I knew very much about Virginia Woolf and her relationship with Vita Sackville-West, to whom this is dedicated. The background is vital because it adds so much and because it helps the reader to reach an understanding of Woolf’s generosity. It is as ever, beautifully written and drifts splendidly through the centuries and the key is Vita and their circle. <br/>As Woolf was writing this her affair with Vita was beginning to wane as Vita was moving on to other l</span>
  <span id="freeText13109657600348353106" style="display:none">I first read this many years ago; before I knew very much about Virginia Woolf and her relationship with Vita Sackville-West, to whom this is dedicated. The background is vital because it adds so much and because it helps the reader to reach an understanding of Woolf’s generosity. It is as ever, beautifully written and drifts splendidly through the centuries and the key is Vita and their circle. <br />As Woolf was writing this her affair with Vita was beginning to wane as Vita was moving on to other lovers. The two women were very different and Vita was much more sexually active and interested in a variety of people. For Vita the thrill of the new was important. Woolf recognised this.<br />One of the keys to the book is Vita’s ancestral home, Knole. It is faithfully represented as Orlando’s home estate in the book, down to the heraldic leopards and the visit of Queen Elizabeth the First. Vita had lost Knole because a woman could not inherit; here Woolf gives her it back.<br />Many of the characters represent people both knew. The Russian princess Sasha is Violet Trefusis, Nicholas Greene is Gosse, Archduchess Harriet/Archduke Henry was Lord Lascelles (one of Vita’s many admirers), Shelmerdine is Vita’s husband Harold Nicholson. Orlando’s poetic work The Oak Tree is equivalent to Vita’s poetic work The Land. <br />There is a great deal of imagery here; some of it in the form of private jokes/codes. The “porpoise in a fishmonger’s shop” is one such (no idea what that one means). The imagery around the goose that crops up a couple of times even confused Vita (Vita was much more literal than Woolf)! It is interesting to consider that originally Woolf had conceived it as an illustrated book with photographs and pictures. Woolf’s portrayal was an accurate one. Harold Nicholson found it difficult to conceive that anyone else could know the private Vita that he knew and thought it was a lucky accident (it wasn’t, Woolf was very perceptive). Mary Campbell (another of Vita’s lovers) was also surprised how accurately the private Vita was portrayed. <br />On top of this being a love letter to Vita, it is so much more besides. The nature of gender and biography are explored. It is also interesting to note that Woolf was also writing the lectures that became A Room of One’s Own. Orlando is part of the train of thought Woolf had about the revolutionary potential of women’s friendship. A new world opens when like each other and are no longer seen as rival’s for men’s affection/approval. <br />It is a tender and humorous love story/letter, almost a faitytale, not meant to be taken in the same vein as more serious work (To The Lighthouse), but it captures the imagination and sold much more than anything Woolf had written previously. It is a work of brilliance with a lightness of touch.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13109657600348353106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_654162733" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/654162733" />
    <a title="Samadrita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13266158-samadrita"><img alt="Samadrita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461258628p2/13266158.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/654162733?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samadrita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samadrita" href="/user/show/13266158-samadrita">Samadrita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156701600" href="/book/show/46133.Orlando">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=britain">britain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=by-women-who-matter">by-women-who-matter</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=timeless-classics">timeless-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=cherished">cherished</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=autobiographical-biography-memoir">autobiographical-biography-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=po-mo">po-mo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=european-literature">european-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=fantasy-mythology">fantasy-mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=humour-of-the-dignified-kind">humour-of-the-dignified-kind</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer654162733" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9867584111136122119">The most prudent way to review a Virginia Woolf book, perhaps, would be to write 'THIS IS STUPENDOUS. GENIUS. AMAZING. WHY HAVEN'T YOU READ THIS YET?' and leave it at that. Because not only does this relieve you of the responsibility of casting about for appropriate words to serenade Woolf but also because you know no review in the world does justice to the sheer magic that she is capable of creating with words.<br/>But since I have a thing for self-flagellation(not really), I wish to undertake preci</span>
  <span id="freeText9867584111136122119" style="display:none">The most prudent way to review a Virginia Woolf book, perhaps, would be to write 'THIS IS STUPENDOUS. GENIUS. AMAZING. WHY HAVEN'T YOU READ THIS YET?' and leave it at that. Because not only does this relieve you of the responsibility of casting about for appropriate words to serenade Woolf but also because you know no review in the world does justice to the sheer magic that she is capable of creating with words.<br />But since I have a thing for self-flagellation(not really), I wish to undertake precisely this mammoth task of writing about Orlando. <br /><br />After having closed the book and put it aside, the first predominant emotions are that of being overwhelmed by the all-encompassing nature of its inherent themes, then awestruck, then of being very close to tears. <br />One is compelled to sit quietly in a corner, still under the heady influence of <i>Orlando</i>'s poetic prose, and brood over all the discrete human sentiments, actions and events that make up life as we know it, letting precious minutes trickle by.<br /><br />Our hero-heroine, Orlando, seems not only to be a representation of the human spirit, a union of <i>yin and yang</i> in all its imperfect glory, but also a lasting testament to the perpetual flow of time. His-her pronouncements sound almost like a chorus of voices, echoing all the dichotomies that characterize our existence and the transience of our emotions.<br />Orlando begins the journey of life as a man of wealth and social standing in Elizabethan era England, comfortable in the skin of his vanity, amorous in his dalliances with women. And the book ends on 11th of October, 1928, in modern England where Orlando is a married woman, a mother, an accomplished writer and finally at peace with life's many ironies and caprices. I will refrain from going into all that takes place between these two distant points in time because for that one can always read the book. <br /><br />It will suffice to say that Orlando swings back and forth between craving and shunning love, between pursuing his-her literary interests and trivializing the urge to write, between seeking the august company of men of letters like Pope, Addison and Swift and then belittling them. And even though hundreds of years pass by as Orlando goes through the many myriad experiences that life had in store for him-her, it seems like everything has remained essentially the same. The reader is struck by a sense of passivity in motion, of an enduring constancy even though the sights and sounds and scenarios, that Orlando flits through, keep varying. <br /><br />Thus in a way <i>Orlando</i> is not different from Woolf's other works just because of the noticeable absence of a stream of consciousness(which, again, is not totally absent here) but because here, she attempts to grasp at an amorphous entity like time and enclose it within a few pages. And I am mightily pleased to say that she pulls off this feat with an elan, one associates only with her. <br />What makes Orlando really stand out among other VW works is the dual gender of its protagonist. Orlando keeps oscillating between his-her manly and womanly bearings and towards the very end, what nullifies the differences between the sexes is his-her humanity, his-her detachment from the material world and a crossover into the realm of the spiritual.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"The whole of her darkened and settled, as when some foil whose addition makes the round and solidity of a surface is added to it, and the shallow becomes deep and the near distant; and all is contained as water is contained by the sides of a well. So she was now darkened, stilled, and become, with the addition of this Orlando, what is called, rightly or wrongly, a single self, a real self."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />The narrative does seem a bit disjointed at certain points, especially when Woolf foregoes conventions and goes into intricate detailing of events which seem of little importance in the greater scheme of things or inserts her witty observations on society's prejudices concerning women, chastity and more.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"Orlando, who was a passionate lover of animals, now noticed that her teeth were crooked and the two front turned inward, which, he said, is a sure sign of a perverse and cruel disposition in women, and so broke the engagement that very night for ever."<br /><br />"I am she that men call Modesty. Virgin I am and ever shall be. Not for me the fruitful fields and the fertile vineyard. Increase is odious to me; and when the apples burgeon or the flocks breed, I run, I run, I let my mantle fall. My hair covers my eyes, I do not see. Spare, O spare!"<br /><br />"Truth come not out from your horrid den. Hide deeper, fearful Truth. For you flaunt in the brutal gaze of the sun things that were better unknown and undone; you unveil the shameful; the dark you make clear, Hide! Hide! Hide!"</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />See what I mean? This is probably Woolf at her funniest and wittiest. So not a single sentence or passage can be devalued even though it may appear a little out of place or slow down the progress of the narrative.<br /><br />In essence, <i>Orlando</i> is a summation of all the irrepressible instincts of both the man and woman - their quest for love and true wisdom, their search for meaning in chaos, their feelings of inferiority aroused by the vastness of the universe and their desire to find an eternity trapped within their brief lifetimes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9867584111136122119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32804270-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564311252p2/32804270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/986347221?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/32804270-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=recs">recs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer986347221" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2594015801236928222">Published in 1928, toward the end of the most productive stage of Woolf's career as a writer, <i>Orlando</i> doubles as national history and romance: the playful and ironic novel famously centers on the transformation of its protagonist's gender, near the start of the 18th century, but most of the story deals with Orlando's different loves and England's changing social norms over the course of three centuries. The gender change and kaleidoscopic setting afford Woolf the chance to examine themes especia</span>
  <span id="freeText2594015801236928222" style="display:none">Published in 1928, toward the end of the most productive stage of Woolf's career as a writer, <i>Orlando</i> doubles as national history and romance: the playful and ironic novel famously centers on the transformation of its protagonist's gender, near the start of the 18th century, but most of the story deals with Orlando's different loves and England's changing social norms over the course of three centuries. The gender change and kaleidoscopic setting afford Woolf the chance to examine themes especially relevant to women in the 1920s, such as bodily autonomy or marriage, from several historical and social vantage points. In contrast to Woolf's other works of fiction, the novel is rather fun to read as well as written in an accessible style, making <i>Orlando</i> the ideal introduction to her later work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2594015801236928222" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Rowena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena"><img alt="Rowena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499471926p2/2990642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rowena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rowena" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena">Rowena</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156031516" href="/book/show/26961.Orlando">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=readalongs">readalongs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=favourite-authors">favourite-authors</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer466289927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6089925370878923014">I absolutely adored this book. The style is definitely different from the other Woolf books I've read so far. What stood out for me was the beautiful use of the language, maybe more than the story. The novel had an almost fairytale-like feel to it, and I was definitely enchanted from the start.<br/><br/>I don't think the following is a spoiler as it is included in the book's blurb : this book is about a 16 year old boy, Orlando, a favourite of Queen Elizabeth I, who one day wakes up to find that he has be</span>
  <span id="freeText6089925370878923014" style="display:none">I absolutely adored this book. The style is definitely different from the other Woolf books I've read so far. What stood out for me was the beautiful use of the language, maybe more than the story. The novel had an almost fairytale-like feel to it, and I was definitely enchanted from the start.<br /><br />I don't think the following is a spoiler as it is included in the book's blurb : this book is about a 16 year old boy, Orlando, a favourite of Queen Elizabeth I, who one day wakes up to find that he has become a woman! The investigation of gender following Orlando’s metamorphosis is especially amazing as now it is widely accepted that gender is a societal construct. I really feel Woolf was way ahead of her time.<br /><br />The book was written in an experimental biographical style, and the biographer threw in a lot of humour and wit that caused me to burst out laughing more than once. It is also satirical which I loved, especially the part where Orlando shows her calf to a sailor, who almost falls to his death!Also, the challenges and insight of writing a biography are included, things I had never really considered previously.<br /><br />The book was so surreal at times especially as it wasn’t restricted by either gender or time. I feel that, as straight-forward as the story is to read, there are so many issues incorporated that I think there are also as many different approaches for reading this book.<br /><br />Now I'm in the mood for more Woolf and I think a re-read of Mrs. Dalloway is in order.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6089925370878923014" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_6898191" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6898191" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6898191?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6898191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18097346361804785253">What's the connection between Virginia Woolf and the Russian mafia? Easy - in 1991 Sally Potter decided to film Orlando, one of the loveliest, most ravishing novels in the English language. Somewheres in the middle of the story there, you have a truly extraordinary sequence about the remarkable Frost Fair of 1654, which was when the River Thames itself froze over and they erected a fair with stalls and games and rides and greased pigs and whatnot on it, a carnival of the utmost brilliancy right </span>
  <span id="freeText18097346361804785253" style="display:none">What's the connection between Virginia Woolf and the Russian mafia? Easy - in 1991 Sally Potter decided to film Orlando, one of the loveliest, most ravishing novels in the English language. Somewheres in the middle of the story there, you have a truly extraordinary sequence about the remarkable Frost Fair of 1654, which was when the River Thames itself froze over and they erected a fair with stalls and games and rides and greased pigs and whatnot on it, a carnival of the utmost brilliancy right on the river itself, and there was skating and flirting and people built fires, right on the river itself, and Orlando cut a dash amongst the Elizabethans and many curious and longing glances were thrown. <br /><br />So Sally Potter needed a frozen river. Where do rivers freeze these days, what with global warming?<br /><br />Kiev. The Dneiper.<br /><br />So they went to Kiev and got permission to film from the newly elected Ukrainian local government. Signed all the forms in triplicate, paid their taxes. Great.<br /><br />But then the hotel door banged open and some big guys came in and said to Sally Potter and her pals<br /><br />We know you have made arrangement with the politicians. Now you must make arrangement with us. <br /><br />Who?<br /><br />Just the boys who really run Kiev, is who.<br /><br />So they paid some more taxes. And didn't ask for a receipt.<br /><br />I remember Sally potter telling this story with great gusto when I saw her introduce this movie at Nottingham's arthouse. In retrospect, she thought it was hilarious. Not while it was happening.<br /><br />Orlando is a lucent multicoloured gleam of a novel, bending the gending a few decades before we even realised that trannies weren't little radios anymore, before we realised that boys will be girls will be boys and that it's a mixed up muddled up shook up world.<br /><br />Except for Orlando. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18097346361804785253" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1221799200" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1221799200" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1221799200?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199536597" href="/book/show/2916109-orlando">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=review-may-contain-comic-content">review-may-contain-comic-content</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=woolf-reviews">woolf-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=woolf-and-related">woolf-and-related</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1221799200" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2660167720592525115">I like nothing better than when two books I happen to be reading overlap, even if briefly, so I was really pleased when Virginia Woolf’s fictional character, Orlando, suddenly mentioned Jonathan Swift, whose <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23267566.The_Journal_to_Stella' title='The Journal to Stella by Jonathan Swift' rel='nofollow noopener'>Journal to Stella</a> I’ve been reading recently. Orlando, who in some sections of Woolf’s book uses the title Lady Orlando, has just been receiving a visit from Joseph Addison, Swift’s one-time bosom pal and fellow political essayist, when there's an interruption: <br/><i>..and when Mr Addison has had </i></span>
  <span id="freeText2660167720592525115" style="display:none">I like nothing better than when two books I happen to be reading overlap, even if briefly, so I was really pleased when Virginia Woolf’s fictional character, Orlando, suddenly mentioned Jonathan Swift, whose <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23267566.The_Journal_to_Stella" title="The Journal to Stella by Jonathan Swift" rel="nofollow noopener">Journal to Stella</a> I’ve been reading recently. Orlando, who in some sections of Woolf’s book uses the title Lady Orlando, has just been receiving a visit from Joseph Addison, Swift’s one-time bosom pal and fellow political essayist, when there's an interruption: <br /><i>..and when Mr Addison has had his say, there is a terrific rap at the door, and Mr Swift, who had these arbitrary ways about him, walks in unannounced...Nothing can be plainer than that violent man. He is so coarse and yet so clean; so brutal, yet so kind; scorns the whole world, yet talks baby language to a girl, and will die, can we doubt it? in a mad house.</i><br /><br />The <i> 'talks baby language to a girl'</i> remark is a direct reference to the letters Swift wrote to his young friend, Esther Johnson, whom he called Stella. Those letters have been incorporated into his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23267566.The_Journal_to_Stella" title="The Journal to Stella by Jonathan Swift" rel="nofollow noopener"> Journal to Stella</a> which I've just been reading. Esther/Stella lived at Swift's house in Ireland with a companion, Mrs Dingley, while Swift spent time in London engaged in politics, pamphleteering, political satire, and visiting Lords and Ladies such as Orlando. Whenever Swift was in Ireland, Esther moved to lodgings nearby for the sake of propriety. There was a rumour that Swift and Esther were secretly married but it is still a rumour, three hundred years later. Esther suffered from ill health, which Swift worried about constantly, and he, though fifteen years older, outlived her by nearly twenty years, suffering from a form of what we now know as Alzheimer's in the end. So not quite, but almost, as Lady Orlando foretold. <br /><br />Swift addressed the letters in the Journal to both Esther and her companion Mrs Dingley, again for the sake of propriety, although neither Esther not Swift could have envisaged them being published. That Woolf's fictional Lady Orlando knew of the contents of the letters even while Swift was writing them should not surprise us; Orlando is a most exotic creation, the ability to see the future only one more surprising trait.<br /><br />But to return to that brief conversation which these two books have had: I felt that Swift should have an opportunity to comment in his turn on Lady Orlando, a kind of quid pro quo as it were, so I couldn't resist creating a <i>
  <b>semi</b>
</i>-fictional letter from Swift’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23267566.The_Journal_to_Stella" title="The Journal to Stella by Jonathan Swift" rel="nofollow noopener"> Journal to Stella</a>. And it serves as a review of Woolf’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18839.Orlando" title="Orlando by Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">Orlando</a> at the same time though more in satire than in conventional review form.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>London, Dec. 23, 1710<br />I have sent my 11th letter tonight as usual and begin the dozenth.<br />I told you I dined at the</b>
</i> Lady Orlando’s, <i>
  <b>and I will tell you no more at present, guess for why; because I am</b>
</i> writing the text of a Proposal I mean to publish, a new theory on the problem of overpopulation and poverty. <i>
  <b>So sit still a while just by me, while I am writing, and don’t say a word, I charge you, and when I am going to bed, I will take you along, and talk a little while, so there, sit there..........................................................................<br />Come then, let us see what we have to say to these saucy brats, that will not let us go sleep at past eleven,</b>
</i> and must have news of the famous Lady Orlando. The last letter was so written over and under and sideways and crossways, that there was no room for the Lady, she being exceedingly tall and in need of more space about her than most. I had told you that Addison had made me an introduction to her before our friendship ended, indeed I met him coming out as I was going to call on her t'other day, and he never spake a word as if I was but the under-butler, and all because I've chosen a different political direction. How cross it made me! But enough of that, <i>
  <b>you know I never write politics to you. Turn over the leaf.</b>
</i><br /><br />To return to the Lady Orlando: 'tis true as you've heard that she is <i>
  <b>curious company,</b>
</i> but still there is in her a <i>
  <b>happy conjunction of civility, freedom, easiness, and sincerity</b>
</i> that I have met but once before; indeed I think that the Lady Orlando and Madam Stella would go on famously together, 'tho perhaps not, and you shall tell me your impressions in your next letter, <i>
  <b>Stellakins.</b>
</i><br /><br />What is certain, in any case, is that the Lady can talk on every subject, seemly or otherwise, and the list of her acquaintances is as long as---the last century, and maybe the next one too. ‘Twere as if she breakfasted but yesterday with Old Bess and supped with Shakespeare the same evening. And though she simpers and smiles enough, she has a sharp eye and a sharper tongue. And when she crosses a room, she leaves a wind in her wake. If it weren’t for the simpers and the skirts, I would say I was nekatsim in the xes, and ‘tis a nam. There now, that’s out, enough to shock the <i>
  <b>saucy sauce boxes</b>
</i> for a month of Satiredays. <br /><br />But thinking about the Lady Orlando has set my mind, nonetheless, on the <i>
  <b>modest proposal</b>
</i> I mentioned above, a proposal that would shew how to keep the population under better control than at present. And the meat of it would be that we breed an entire race of Orlandos as would live forever without need of further propagation. <i>
  <b>Are you bit, or are you not, sirrahs?</b>
</i> These wo-men would be capable of switching from one sex to t’other according as the times required - men for battle, women when we’d no need of soldiers. Nay, I hear Stella’s voice, chiding me, saying there’d be no little children in such a world. But then there’d be no lying-in, and no dying of it, and a pox of other ills besides that are not for <i>
  <b>little rogues’ ears, sirrahs</b>
</i>!<br /> <br />And think on the accumulation of knowledge there would be in such an age, where nothing was forgotten but everything known at the same time by the same minds. For these wo-men’s minds would be all of the same cast, as I’m inclined to think they are already, though ‘tis little acknowledged. They’d all read Latin and Greek and all write verses as a pastime. And there would be no need for governance since they would all be equally educated and wise; what a saving in scribes and sealing wax!<br />So there you have it, my modest proposal, though some will say 'tis more immodest than any I’ve made before!<br /><i>
  <b>Paaast twelve o’clock and so good-night, myownlittledearsaucyinsolentrogues.<br />Well, but this is a long one? <br />No, sirrahs, I warrant you: too long for naughty girls. <br />Go, sauceboxes, good-night.</b>
</i><br /><br />………………………………………………………………<br />Swift did write <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5206937.A_Modest_Proposal" title="A Modest Proposal by Jonathan Swift" rel="nofollow noopener">A Modest Proposal</a>: <i>for preventing the children of poor people in Ireland from being a burden on their parents and the country, and for making them beneficial to the publick.</i><br />That proposal was much more outrageous than the one I've invented here!<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[He proposed that the children be sold at one year old to the wealthy to be served up, cooked, at the table, providing a profitable deal for their parents since babies under a year don't even require solid feeding. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="2660167720592525115" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109006234" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/118558-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277521087p2/118558.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109006234?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/118558-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/118558-sarah?shelf=lgbtqa">lgbtqa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/118558-sarah?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/118558-sarah?shelf=50book-challenge">50book-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/118558-sarah?shelf=classics">classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109006234" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2760532651028368928">Vita Sackville-West's son may have called <i>Orlando</i> “the longest and most charming love-letter in literature”, but let me tell you: if someone wrote me a love letter like this, their ass would be getting dumped shortly thereafter. <br/><br/>This book was like the song that wouldn't end- it just goes on and on (yet it isn't particularly lengthy) without saying very much of interest. Despite the fact that reading it was a serious chore, for whatever reason I couldn't just give up and toss it aside (much like </span>
  <span id="freeText2760532651028368928" style="display:none">Vita Sackville-West's son may have called <i>Orlando</i> “the longest and most charming love-letter in literature”, but let me tell you: if someone wrote me a love letter like this, their ass would be getting dumped shortly thereafter. <br /><br />This book was like the song that wouldn't end- it just goes on and on (yet it isn't particularly lengthy) without saying very much of interest. Despite the fact that reading it was a serious chore, for whatever reason I couldn't just give up and toss it aside (much like being unable to look away from a flaming car wreck). I pushed through, even though I often couldn't bring myself to read more than a few pages at a time. It took me several weeks to finish.<br /><br />However odd the movie was (and in spite of the fact I have the distinct impression that Tilda Swinton wanted to eat my soul), I still enjoyed it more than the book (which is not a statement I make lightly- I almost never like a movie more than its source material).<br /><br />I think part of the problem was the fact that Orlando was a boring, whiny, immature character who took hundreds of years to grow up. Since the book is pretty much entirely about Orlando, if one is not a fan, the book is not exactly fun to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2760532651028368928" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer117148187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8879292687002927493">I finished this book about a week ago, and have been trying ever since to figure out how I'm supposed to review it. I honestly can't think of anything to say except this:<br/><br/>Every single emotion I've ever felt and every thought I've ever had, had already been felt and thought and written down by Virginia Woolf decades before I was even born. There is not a single concept or feeling in any of her books that isn't already intimately familiar to me. Reading her books is like having someone look into my</span>
  <span id="freeText8879292687002927493" style="display:none">I finished this book about a week ago, and have been trying ever since to figure out how I'm supposed to review it. I honestly can't think of anything to say except this:<br /><br />Every single emotion I've ever felt and every thought I've ever had, had already been felt and thought and written down by Virginia Woolf decades before I was even born. There is not a single concept or feeling in any of her books that isn't already intimately familiar to me. Reading her books is like having someone look into my own mind, deeper than I ever looked, and discovering something that is simultaneously unheard of and completely recognizable. </span>

<div id="review_113860665" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113860665" />
    <a title="Mir" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/237469-mir"><img alt="Mir" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1275489063p2/237469.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/113860665?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mir" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mir" href="/user/show/237469-mir">Mir</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer113860665" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10127872592418806507"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18839.Orlando' title='Orlando by Virginia Woolf' rel='nofollow noopener'>Orlando</a> was much funnier than I expected, and much less fantastical. Since I was familiar with the plot before beginning the book and had heard much literary criticism concerning the famed transformation, I was expecting the focus to be on gender issues. While these were certainly present, Woolf presents them fairly gently. Orlando is so strongly an individual that his/her sex hardly matters from a readerly standpoint. Indeed, I found it harder to believe that he was a successful ambassador than</span>
  <span id="freeText10127872592418806507" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18839.Orlando" title="Orlando by Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">Orlando</a> was much funnier than I expected, and much less fantastical. Since I was familiar with the plot before beginning the book and had heard much literary criticism concerning the famed transformation, I was expecting the focus to be on gender issues. While these were certainly present, Woolf presents them fairly gently. Orlando is so strongly an individual that his/her sex hardly matters from a readerly standpoint. Indeed, I found it harder to believe that he was a successful ambassador than that he became a woman. But we don't see Orlando being an ambassador, and hardly more do we see her being a literary hostess. Despite the pretense at biography, we are really inside Orlando's head, experiencing a thought process and a personality rather than actions. To be sure, many people probably seem quite different inside their heads than in our workaday experience of them.<br /><br />My favorite aspect of this novel was the commentary on writing, most of which is simply hilarious. The depictions of Orlando's struggles with creativity showcase Woolf's talent for combining painfully astute mockery with personal sympathy, and the snippets she uses to illustrate the styles of various periods are perfect. The scene where Orlando finds herself helplessly writing missish verse is a side-splitter. <i><br /><blockquote>She was so changed, the soft carnation cloud<br />Once mantling o'er her cheek like that which eve<br />Hangs o'er the sky, glowing with roseate hue,<br />Had faded into paleness, broken by <br />Bright burning blushes, torches of the tomb,<br /></blockquote><br />but here, by an abrupt movement she spilt the ink over the page and blotted it from human sight she hoped for ever.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10127872592418806507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_193913031" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>

        rated it

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19893143-orlando">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=british">british</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer193913031" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16907960285258317068"><i>"The change of sex, though it altered their future, did nothing whatever to alter their identity."</i><br/>- Virginia Woolf, Orlando<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445259977i/16622145._SY540_.jpg' width='400' height='534' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>A beautiful, poetic look at gender, sex, poetry, time, love, living, etc. This gender-studies masterpiece was inspired by Woolf's reltionship with Vita Sackville-West. According to Vita's son: <i>"The effect of Vita on Virginia is all contained in Orlando, the longest and most charming love letter in literature, in which she explores Vita, weaves her in and out of the centuries</i></span>
  <span id="freeText16907960285258317068" style="display:none"><i>"The change of sex, though it altered their future, did nothing whatever to alter their identity."</i><br>- Virginia Woolf, Orlando<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445259977i/16622145._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="534" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A beautiful, poetic look at gender, sex, poetry, time, love, living, etc. This gender-studies masterpiece was inspired by Woolf's reltionship with Vita Sackville-West. According to Vita's son: <i>"The effect of Vita on Virginia is all contained in Orlando, the longest and most charming love letter in literature, in which she explores Vita, weaves her in and out of the centuries, tosses her from one sex to the other, plays with her, dresses her in furs, lace and emeralds, teases her, flirts with her, drops a veil of mist around her."</i><br><br>This is one of the easier Woolf novels to read. Toward the end it gets a bit stream-of-consious (as Modern writers were wont to do), but the narrative of this novel floats and folds through time and gender easily. It was an amazing way of looking at the female experience because Orlando's experience was first fundamentally experienced by Orlando regardless of their gender. So, the novel as biography allowed Woolf to mine the experience from the inside instead of the outside. It also allowed the fluidity of gender to be explored in a way that a less fanciful novel might not have been able to.<br><br>It is wild to think this book was published in 1928 AND here we are 90-years-later still working our britches into Puritanical bunches over gender and bathrooms. What a bunch of nonsense. One area of hope does exist. In my lifetime, I have seen a huge increase in the attention paid to the difficulties faced by those who don't fit into the gender norms. Things ARE SLOWLY getting better for my friends and the children of my friends who might not fit easily into the pants or skirts society wants to drop them in. Hopefully, it doesn't take 400 years. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16907960285258317068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_762091134" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/762091134" />
    <a title="Rakhi Dalal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3655942-rakhi-dalal"><img alt="Rakhi Dalal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557596257p2/3655942.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/762091134?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rakhi Dalal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rakhi Dalal" href="/user/show/3655942-rakhi-dalal">Rakhi Dalal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10694074-orlando">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655942-rakhi-dalal?shelf=woolf">woolf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655942-rakhi-dalal?shelf=bloomsbury">bloomsbury</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655942-rakhi-dalal?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer762091134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1655703140527370980">As always, Woolf has stunned me with the magic of her prose here. Telling this isn’t important, neither that it is a biography; that it informs us about the affair of Vita and Violet. I guess much has been said about that. When I started reading, I had no idea about the references to people, places, their characters or their lives as are known to be mentioned in this work. In fact, as the novel proceeded from Orlando’s gender change for the first time, I had a notion about the invisible layer of</span>
  <span id="freeText1655703140527370980" style="display:none">As always, Woolf has stunned me with the magic of her prose here. Telling this isn’t important, neither that it is a biography; that it informs us about the affair of Vita and Violet. I guess much has been said about that. When I started reading, I had no idea about the references to people, places, their characters or their lives as are known to be mentioned in this work. In fact, as the novel proceeded from Orlando’s gender change for the first time, I had a notion about the invisible layer of narrative which Virginia had experimented with, in this work. And as the work moved through centuries, I realized that the notion was making sense. <br /><br />I didn’t even read it as something related to gender issues, though they might as well have been mentioned or portrayed deliberately, specially with reference to Pope, Addison and Swift. <br /><br />To me, Orlando, in its truest sense renders the spirit of literature of the respective times it refers to, as it proceeds in time. The different centuries starting from Elizabethan and Jacobean to Restoration, Augustan, to Age of Sensibility, Romanticism and finally to Victorian, have been depicted in the form of exploration of the human mind. In any age, as it holds true, the people are influenced by the spirit of the age, which in turn reflects in the literature of the respective age, so Orlando is one finest description of Zeitgeist. <br /><br />Orlando’s love affair with Sasha symbolizes the passionate extremism of Elizabethan period, while his engagement with reading and writing poetry attributes to characteristics of Jacobean period. During Restoration period, Orlando is sent as an ambassador to Constantinople, whereas the Age of Enlightenment or Augustan lets her see right through the poets like Pope, Addison and Swift and makes her wonder at their foibles. In nineteenth century or the age of realism, Orlando, as a woman, realizes that she needs to marry to secure her social standing. Her marriage to Shelmerdine might actually be a depiction of the European Romantic movement reaching America in early nineteenth century.<br /><br />The Victorian age, witness the coming of Orlando in terms of herself as a woman or a writer and thus represents the increased role of women as a reader as well as a writer during that age. And the present time i.e. 11th October 1928, when the novel is published, we observe that Orlando is happy with the changing times, that she has finally arrived there, which cannot be neglected for the fact that the work was published when Woolf’s writing was at its height in terms of its popularity.<br /><br />I am in awe of Woolf for her eagerness and will to experiment with the style of prose and her aversion to the well accepted norms of written word during her times. A profound expression of her ideas about how to write, what to write and for whom to write i.e. the relationship between a writer and a reader, can be witnessed in her essay Mr. Bennett and Mrs. Brown. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1655703140527370980" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1783477462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1783477462" />

<div id="review_2483219016" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2483219016" />
    <a title="MihaElla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36408043-mihaella"><img alt="MihaElla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596985417p2/36408043.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2483219016?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MihaElla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MihaElla" href="/user/show/36408043-mihaella">MihaElla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9786068255569" href="/book/show/17913944-orlando">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2483219016" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer240481221971359144">Maybe not the very first but still my very first seriously engaged meeting with Woolf V. is undoubtedly successful. I picked Orlando after dropping - temporarily and until I get a more senseful sense of her works - its Essays. I was charmed by the language and its expressions but, in parallel, I was aware that nothing of the content sticks with me, because whatever she was saying as for arguments, debating facts, extensive monologues, was really foreign language to me, although I was reading it </span>
  <span id="freeText240481221971359144" style="display:none">Maybe not the very first but still my very first seriously engaged meeting with Woolf V. is undoubtedly successful. I picked Orlando after dropping - temporarily and until I get a more senseful sense of her works - its Essays. I was charmed by the language and its expressions but, in parallel, I was aware that nothing of the content sticks with me, because whatever she was saying as for arguments, debating facts, extensive monologues, was really foreign language to me, although I was reading it in Romanian translation.<br />So, Orlando came by sequence, considering that other two of her works for which I own a paper copy, The Waves and To the Lighthouse, should be held for a later moment, more precisely when on my new summer vacation, which is actually due as for today onwards...not yet there, at destination, but it shall be completed during the present day.<br />Orlando was a really joyful read. I wasn't familiar with the author, actually I wasn't having a sort of framework of expecting something, therefore I was having no scenarios of what could be about. I read, as usual, the back cover of the paper copy but the text was rendering some ideas that didn't ring any connection with something that I might have known about. <br />For all the sparkles that came alongside the pages' turning, it made me clearly remember the joy of reading as if savouring with high gusto a most wanted food. It was like feeling that I do agree with whatever you write about, you really awesome writer. Whatever you write, say, feel, think, is enough by itself and surely something to be kept as a cherished gem. It didn't even count fiction/ non-fiction, theme/subject-wise, participants/non-participants, etc. Reading as such filled a piece of a little void somewhere inside my mind's chambers. <br />Initially I wanted to write about my fuller impressions on Orlando immediately that I finished it. But late last evening my attention was more firmly caught by some information broadcasting on TV about an ongoing event in Bucharest. During the day and continuing until after mid night it was held a Diaspora meeting. Voices were spoken before that this event will not be peaceful and within law. It was not. But this is not what disturbed me. Around 23:00, when the gathering was already infused by street convulsions and some small groups of hooligans tried to attack and trigger into confrontation the police force, I saw plainly and straightfoward how this one group of hooligans managed to detach two gendarmes from their comrades, surrounded them and beat them like hell. One of them is a very young, 20 years, lady-gendarme. She was taken off her head protection and put on the ground and a mass of 20-30 hooligans was over her, almost killing her. She is now in the hospital fighting for her life, might remain paralyzed because of broken back bones.<br />The words cannot render the horror of it. It chased me during the few hours I slept, I woke up with the same feeling. If it were a group of animals attacking other animals for food, might have been explained as life survival. But this was not the case. It were so called humans living in a so called civilized century, pretending to be so called civilized humans. <br />Maybe as Orlando felt, nothing much changed during 300-400 years of existence. <br />Will it ever change something? As it looks, chances are small, almost hard to grasp.</span>
  <a data-text-id="240481221971359144" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
